   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Micrometeorite
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
       <caption>
        Micrometeorite
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Micrometeorite.jpg">
          <img alt="Micrometeorite.jpg" data-file-height="178" data-file-width="300" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Micrometeorite.jpg/220px-Micrometeorite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Micrometeorite.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
         </a>
         <div>
          Micrometerorite collected from the
          <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">
           Antarctic
          </a>
          snow.
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       A
       <b>
        micrometeorite
       </b>
       is essentially a
       <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">
        micrometeoroid
       </a>
       that has survived
       <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">
        entry
       </a>
       through
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">
        Earth's atmosphere
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Particle_size" title="Particle size">
        size
       </a>
       of such a
       <a href="/wiki/Particle" title="Particle">
        particle
       </a>
       ranges from 50
       <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">
        µm
       </a>
       to 2
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millimeter" title="Millimeter">
        mm
       </a>
       . Usually found on
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       's surface, micrometeorites differ from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorites" title="Meteorites">
        meteorites
       </a>
       in that they are smaller in size, more abundant, and different in composition. They are a subset of
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">
        cosmic dust
       </a>
       , which also includes the smaller
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust" title="Interplanetary dust">
        interplanetary dust
       </a>
       particles (IDPs).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brownlee1997_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brownlee1997-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Micrometeorites enter Earth's atmosphere at high
       <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">
        velocities
       </a>
       (at least 11 km/s) and undergo heating through
       <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">
        atmospheric friction
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Compression_(physics)" title="Compression (physics)">
        compression
       </a>
       . Micrometeorites individually weigh between 10
       <sup>
        −9
       </sup>
       and 10
       <sup>
        −4
       </sup>
       g and collectively comprise most of the extraterrestrial material that has come to the present-day Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Love_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Love-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Fred_Lawrence_Whipple" title="Fred Lawrence Whipple">
        Fred Lawrence Whipple
       </a>
       first coined the term "micro-meteorite" to describe dust-sized objects that fall to the Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whipple_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Whipple-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sometimes meteoroids and micrometeoroids entering the Earth's atmosphere are visible as
       <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid#Meteor" title="Meteoroid">
        meteors or "shooting stars"
       </a>
       , whether or not they reach the ground and survive as meteorites and micrometorites.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>
      <div class="toc" id="toc">
       <div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en" xml:lang="en">

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">
        Introduction
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Introduction">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Micrometeorite (MM) textures vary as their original structural and mineral compositions are modified by the degree of heating that they experience entering the atmosphere—a function of their initial speed and angle of entry. They range from unmelted particles that retain their original
       <a href="/wiki/Mineralogy" title="Mineralogy">
        mineralogy
       </a>
       (Fig. 1 a, b), to partially melted particles (Fig. 1 c, d) to round melted cosmic spherules (Fig. 1 e, f, g, h, Fig. 2) some of which have lost a large portion of their mass through vaporization (Fig. 1 i). Classification is based on composition and degree of heating.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genge_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Genge-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cross_sections_of_different_micrometeorite_classes.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1761" data-file-width="2334" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Cross_sections_of_different_micrometeorite_classes.jpg/250px-Cross_sections_of_different_micrometeorite_classes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Cross_sections_of_different_micrometeorite_classes.jpg/375px-Cross_sections_of_different_micrometeorite_classes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Cross_sections_of_different_micrometeorite_classes.jpg/500px-Cross_sections_of_different_micrometeorite_classes.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cross_sections_of_different_micrometeorite_classes.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Figure 1. Cross sections of different micrometeorite classes: a) Fine-grained unmelted; b) Coarse-grained Unmelted; c) Scoriaceous; d) Relict-grain Bearing; e) Porphyritic; f) Barred olivine; g) Cryptocrystalline; h) Glass; i) CAT; j) G-type; k) I-type; and l) Single mineral. Except for G- and I-types all are silicate rich, called stony MMs. Scale bars are 50µm.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Light_microscope_images_of_stony_cosmic_spherules.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="621" data-file-width="815" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Light_microscope_images_of_stony_cosmic_spherules.png/250px-Light_microscope_images_of_stony_cosmic_spherules.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Light_microscope_images_of_stony_cosmic_spherules.png/375px-Light_microscope_images_of_stony_cosmic_spherules.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Light_microscope_images_of_stony_cosmic_spherules.png/500px-Light_microscope_images_of_stony_cosmic_spherules.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Light_microscope_images_of_stony_cosmic_spherules.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Figure 2. Light microscope images of stony cosmic spherules.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The extraterrestrial origins of micrometeorites are determined by microanalyses that show that:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        The metal they contain is similar to that found in meteorites.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smales_6-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Smales-6">
          [6]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Some have
        <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCstite" title="Wüstite">
         wüstite
        </a>
        , a high-temperature iron oxide found in meteorite fusion crusts.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marvin_7-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Marvin-7">
          [7]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Their silicate minerals have major and trace elements ratios similar to those in meteorites.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blanchard_8-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Blanchard-8">
          [8]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ganapathy_9-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Ganapathy-9">
          [9]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The abundances of cosmogenic manganese (
        <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_manganese" title="Isotopes of manganese">
         <sup>
          53
         </sup>
         Mn
        </a>
        ) in iron spherules and of cosmogenic beryllium (
        <a href="/wiki/Beryllium-10" title="Beryllium-10">
         <sup>
          10
         </sup>
         Be
        </a>
        ), aluminum (
        <a href="/wiki/Aluminium-26" title="Aluminium-26">
         <sup>
          26
         </sup>
         Al
        </a>
        ), and solar
        <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">
         neon
        </a>
        isotope in stony MMs are extraterrestrial
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raisbeck_10-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Raisbeck-10">
          [10]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nishiizumi_11-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Nishiizumi-11">
          [11]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The presence of pre-solar grains in some MMs
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yada_12-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Yada-12">
          [12]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
         deuterium
        </a>
        excesses in ultra-carbonaceous MMs
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duprat_13-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Duprat-13">
          [13]
         </a>
        </sup>
        indicates that they are not only extraterrestrial but that some of their components formed before our
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">
         solar system
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       An estimated 30,000 ± 20,000
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tonnes" title="Tonnes">
        tonnes
       </a>
       per year (t/yr)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Love_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Love-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of cosmic dust enters the upper atmosphere each year of which less than 10% (2700 ± 1400 t/yr) is estimated to reach the surface as particles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor1998_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taylor1998-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Therefore, the mass of micrometeorites deposited is roughly 50 times higher than that estimated for meteorites, which represent approximately 50 t/yr,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zolensky_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zolensky-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the huge number of particles entering the atmosphere each year (~10
       <sup>
        17
       </sup>
       &gt; 10 µm) suggests that large MM collections contain particles from all dust producing objects in the Solar System including asteroids, comets, and fragments from our Moon and Mars. Large MM collections provide information on the size, composition, atmospheric heating effects and types of materials accreting on Earth while detailed studies of individual MMs give insights into their origin, the nature of the
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
        carbon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">
        amino acids
       </a>
       and pre-solar grains they contain.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Collection_sites">
        Collection sites
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Collection sites">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Micrometeorites have been collected from deep-sea
       <a href="/wiki/Sediment" title="Sediment">
        sediments
       </a>
       , sedimentary rocks and polar sediments; they are currently collected primarily from polar snow and ice. Because of their low concentrations on the Earth's surface, MMs are sought in environments that concentrate these materials relative to terrestrial particles.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_sediments">
        Ocean sediments
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ocean sediments">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Melted micrometeorites (cosmic spherules) were first collected from deep-sea sediments during the 1873 to 1876
       <a href="/wiki/Challenger_expedition" title="Challenger expedition">
        expedition of the
        <i>
         HMS Challenger
        </i>
       </a>
       . In 1891, Murray and Renard found "two groups [of micrometeorites]: first, black magnetic spherules, with or without a metallic nucleus; second, brown-coloured spherules resembling chondr(ul)es, with a crystalline structure".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray_&amp;_Renard_1891_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Murray_&amp;_Renard_1891-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1883, they suggested that these spherules were extraterrestrial because they were found far from terrestrial particle sources, they did not resemble magnetic spheres produced in furnaces of the time, and their
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel-iron_alloy" title="Nickel-iron alloy">
        nickel-iron
       </a>
       (Fe-Ni) metal cores did not resemble metallic iron found in volcanic rocks. The spherules were most abundant in slowly accumulating sediments, particularly red clays deposited below the
       <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_compensation_depth" title="Carbonate compensation depth">
        carbonate compensation depth
       </a>
       , a finding that supported a meteoritic origin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Murray_&amp;_Renard_1883_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Murray_&amp;_Renard_1883-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition to those spheres with Fe-Ni metal cores, some spherules larger than 300 µm contain a core of elements from the platinum group.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the first collection of the
       <i>
        HMS Challenger
       </i>
       , cosmic spherules have been recovered from ocean sediments using cores, box cores, clamshell grabbers, and magnetic sleds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Among these a magnetic sled, called the "Cosmic Muck Rake", retrieved thousands of cosmic spherules from the top 10 cm of red clays on the
       <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">
        Pacific Ocean
       </a>
       floor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Terrestrial_sediments">
        Terrestrial sediments
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Terrestrial sediments">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Terrestrial sediments also contain micrometeorites. These have been found in samples that:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Have low
        <a href="/wiki/Sedimentation" title="Sedimentation">
         sedimentation
        </a>
        rates such as claystones
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
         <a href="#cite_note-22">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardgrounds" title="Hardgrounds">
         hardgrounds
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czajkowski_23-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Czajkowski-23">
          [23]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
         <a href="#cite_note-24">
          [24]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Are easily dissolved such as
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_deposit" title="Salt deposit">
         salt deposits
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
         <a href="#cite_note-25">
          [25]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">
         limestones
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
         <a href="#cite_note-26">
          [26]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Have been mass sorted such as heavy mineral concentrates found in deserts
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
         <a href="#cite_note-27">
          [27]
         </a>
        </sup>
        and beach sands.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marvin_7-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Marvin-7">
          [7]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The oldest MMs are totally altered iron spherules found in 140- to 180-million-year-old hardgrounds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Czajkowski_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Czajkowski-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Amateur collectors may find micrometeorites in areas where dirt and dust from a large area has been concentrated, such as from a roof downspout.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_depositions">
        Polar depositions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Polar depositions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:250px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:400px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/SPWW.webmhd.webm/400px--SPWW.webmhd.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:400px;height:300px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="409.792" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="SPWW.webmhd.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/SPWW.webmhd.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Original WebM file, 400 × 300 (584 kbps)" data-shorttitle="WebM source" data-width="400" data-height="300" data-bandwidth="584389" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c4/SPWW.webmhd.webm/SPWW.webmhd.webm.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="214" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="212288" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/c/c4/SPWW.webmhd.webm/SPWW.webmhd.webm.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="320" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="322064" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'>
         <img alt="File:SPWW.webmhd.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/SPWW.webmhd.webm/250px--SPWW.webmhd.webm.jpg" style="width:250px;height:188px"/>
         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/SPWW.webmhd.webm" target="new" title="Play media">
          <span class="play-btn-large">
           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
            Play media
           </span>
          </span>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SPWW.webmhd.webm" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Click here to see a seven-minute movie of MMs being collected from the bottom of the South Pole drinking water well.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Micrometeorites found in polar sediments are much less weathered than those found in other terrestrial environments, as evidenced by little etching of interstitial glass, and the presence of large numbers of glass spherules and unmelted micrometeorites, particle types that are rare or absent in deep-sea samples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The MMs found in polar regions have been collected from Greenland snow,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Greenland cryoconite,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Antarctic blue ice
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Antarctic aeolian (wind-driven) debris,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koeberl_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Koeberl-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hagen_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hagen-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvey_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harvey-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ice cores,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yiou_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Yiou-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the bottom of the South Pole water well,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2000_4-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taylor2000-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor1998_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taylor1998-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Antarctic sediment traps
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and present day Antarctic snow.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duprat_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Duprat-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_and_origins_of_micrometeorites">
        Classification and origins of micrometeorites
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classification and origins of micrometeorites">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Modern
       <a href="/wiki/Meteorite#Meteorite_types" title="Meteorite">
        classification of meteorites
       </a>
       and micrometeorites is complex; the 2007 review paper of Krot et al.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krot:2007_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Krot:2007-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       summarizes modern meteorite taxonomy. Linking individual micrometeorites to meteorite classification groups requires a comparison of their elemental, isotopic and textural characteristics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genge_2008_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Genge_2008-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Comet_vs_asteroid_origin_of_micrometeorites">
        Comet vs asteroid origin of micrometeorites
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Comet vs asteroid origin of micrometeorites">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Whereas most meteorites likely originate from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroids" title="Asteroids">
        asteroids
       </a>
       , the contrasting makeup of micrometeorites suggests that most originate from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">
        comets
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Fewer than 1% of MMs are
       <a href="/wiki/Achondrite" title="Achondrite">
        achondritic
       </a>
       and are similar to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HED_meteorites" title="HED meteorites">
        HED meteorites
       </a>
       , which are thought to be from the asteroid,
       <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">
        4 Vesta
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most MMs are compositionally similar to
       <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">
        carbonaceous chondrites
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whereas approximately 3% of meteorites are of this type.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The dominance of carbonaceous chondrite-like MMs and their low abundance in meteorite collections suggests that most MMs derive from sources different than those for most meteorites. Since most meteorites probably derive from asteroids, an alternative source for MMs might be comets. The idea that MMs might originate from comets originated in 1950.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whipple_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Whipple-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Until recently the greater-than-25-km/s entry velocities of micrometeoroids, measured for particles from comet streams, cast doubts against their survival as MMs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raisbeck_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Raisbeck-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, recent dynamical simulations
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       suggest that 85% of cosmic dust could be cometary. Furthermore, analyses of particles returned from the comet,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wild_2" title="Wild 2">
        Wild 2
       </a>
       , by the
       <a href="/wiki/Stardust_(spacecraft)" title="Stardust (spacecraft)">
        <i>
         Stardust
        </i>
        spacecraft
       </a>
       show that these particles have compositions that are consistent with many micrometeorites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Nonetheless, some parent bodies of micrometeorites appear to be asteroids with
       <a href="/wiki/Chondrule" title="Chondrule">
        chondrule
       </a>
       -bearing carbonaceous
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chondrites" title="Chondrites">
        chondrites
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genge_2005_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Genge_2005-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_micrometeorites">
        Extraterrestrial micrometeorites
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Micrometeorite&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Extraterrestrial micrometeorites">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The influx of micrometeoroids also contributes to the composition of
       <a href="/wiki/Regolith" title="Regolith">
        regolith
       </a>
       (planetary/lunar soil) on other bodies in the Solar System.
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       has an estimated annual micrometeoroid influx of between 2,700 and 59,000 t/yr. This contributes to about 1m of micrometeoritic content to the depth of the Martian regolith every billion years. Measurements from the
       <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">
        Viking program
       </a>
       indicate that the Martian regolith is composed of 60% basaltic rock and 40% rock of meteoritic origin. The lower-density Martian atmosphere allows much larger particles than on Earth to survive the passage through to the surface, largely unaltered until impact. While on Earth particles that survive entry typically have undergone significant transformation, a significant fraction of particles entering the Martian atmosphere throughout the 60 to 1200-μm diameter range probably survive unmelted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flynn_1990_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Flynn_1990-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>

      <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
       <ol class="references">
        <li id="cite_note-Brownlee1997-1">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Brownlee1997_1-0">
            ^
           </a>
